        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:12-05-08_AS1.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Andasol_Solar_Power_Station" title="Andasol Solar Power Station">
          Andasol Solar Power Station
         </a>
         , in
         <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
          Spain
         </a>
         , exploits energy constantly sent to
         <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
          Earth
         </a>
         , free of charge, by the great
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fusion_reactor" title="Fusion reactor">
          fusion reactor
         </a>
         in the sky, the
         <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
          Sun
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Meanwhile, ancient and populous Asian civilizations,
       <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">
        India
       </a>
       and especially
       <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
        China
       </a>
       , have been emerging from centuries of relative scientific, technological, and economic languishment to become potential rivals for Western, chiefly European and United States, economic and political ascendance in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg/220px-Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg/330px-Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg/440px-Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wind_farm_xinjiang.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">
          Wind-power farm
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
          China
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Worldwide
       <a href="/wiki/Demand" title="Demand">
        demand
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Competition" title="Competition">
        competition
       </a>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">
        resources
       </a>
       has risen due to growing populations and industrialization, especially in India, China, and Brazil (see
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions_per_capita" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita">
        List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions per capita
       </a>
       ). This increased demand is causing increased levels of environmental degradation and a fast-growing threat of disastrous
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
       . That, and a need for reliable energy supplies independent of politically volatile regions, has spurred the development of alternative,
       <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">
        renewable
       </a>
       sources of
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energy
       </a>
       (notably
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">
        solar energy
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">
        wind energy
       </a>
       ), proposals for cleaner
       <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">
        fossil-fuel
       </a>
       technologies
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and for expanded use of
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">
        nuclear energy
       </a>
       (somewhat dampened by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accidents#Nuclear_power_plant_accidents" title="Nuclear accidents">
        nuclear-plant accidents
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and, conversely, calls to eschew the indiscriminate large-scale employment of the "fissile-fossil complex" of fissile- (nuclear) and fossil-fuel (coal, petroleum, natural-gas) energy generation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In recognition that
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
       caused by growing concentrations of man-made
       <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
        greenhouse gases
       </a>
       was an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Existential_threat" title="Existential threat">
        existential threat
       </a>
       to everyone on Earth,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [d]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in December 2015 195 countries signed the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Climate_Agreement" title="Paris Climate Agreement">
        Paris Climate Agreement
       </a>
       , scheduled to go into effect in 2020. Thus with the exception of two non-signatory countries (and in June 2017, after the fact, with the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_withdrawal_from_the_Paris_Agreement" title="United States withdrawal from the Paris Agreement">
        exception of the United States
       </a>
       government) all the world's countries explicitly recognized that this common existential threat required a common cooperative response. The transition to environmentally sustainable energy is being aided by the growing economic competitiveness of
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">
        solar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">
        wind energy
       </a>
       vis-à-vis the fissile-fossil complex of
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">
        nuclear
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_power_station" title="Fossil fuel power station">
        fossil-fuel energy
       </a>
       .
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-46">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-46">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The very word "
           <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">
            civilization
           </a>
           " comes from the
           <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
            Latin
           </a>
           <i>
            civilis
           </i>
           , meaning "civil," related to
           <i>
            civis
           </i>
           ("citizen") and
           <i>
            civitas
           </i>
           ("city" or "city-state").
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
            <a href="#cite_note-45">
             [45]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-112">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-112">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           "Early Modern," historically speaking, refers to Western European history from 1501 (after the widely accepted end of the
           <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">
            Late Middle Ages
           </a>
           ; the transition period was the 15th century) to either 1750 or c. 1790–1800, by whichever
           <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(reference_date)" title="Epoch (reference date)">
            epoch
           </a>
           is favored by a school of scholars defining the period—which, in many cases of
           <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">
            periodization
           </a>
           , differs as well within a discipline such as art, philosophy or history.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-113">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-113">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The Age of Enlightenment has also been referred to as the
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Reason" title="Age of Reason">
            Age of Reason
           </a>
           . Historians also include the late 17th century, which is typically known as the Age of Reason or
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Rationalism" title="Age of Rationalism">
            Age of Rationalism
           </a>
           , as part of the Enlightenment; however, contemporary historians have considered the Age of Reason distinct to the ideas developed in the Enlightenment. The use of the term here includes both Ages under a single all-inclusive time-frame.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-150">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-150">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The "
           <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">
            greenhouse effect
           </a>
           ", substantially responsible for Earth's
           <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
            global warming
           </a>
           , was first described in 1824 by the French mathematician
           <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Fourier" title="Joseph Fourier">
            Joseph Fourier
           </a>
           . The greenhouse effect is a natural mechanism that becomes dangerous if the
           <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
            atmosphere
           </a>
           's
           <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
            greenhouse-gas
           </a>
           concentrations exceed environmentally safe levels, as they have gradually been doing since the start of the
           <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">
            Industrial Revolution
           </a>
           . As predicted, this is already increasing the frequency and severity of
           <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">
            floods
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">
            droughts
           </a>
           due to accelerated melting of
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icecap" title="Icecap">
            icecaps
           </a>
           ,
           <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">
            glaciers
           </a>
           , and
           <a href="/wiki/Snowpack" title="Snowpack">
            snowpacks
           </a>
           ; flooding of low-lying
           <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">
            coasts
           </a>
           ;
           <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">
            storms
           </a>
           ;
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural" title="Agricultural">
            agricultural
           </a>
           disruption and
           <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">
            famines
           </a>
           ;
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological" title="Ecological">
            ecological
           </a>
           displacements;
           <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">
            ocean acidification
           </a>
           with havoc to marine life; release, from thawing
           <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
            Arctic
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">
            permafrost
           </a>
           , of
           <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
            methane
           </a>
           , a greenhouse gas more powerful than
           <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
            carbon dioxide
           </a>
           ; and intra- and intersocietal
           <a href="/wiki/Conflict_(process)" title="Conflict (process)">
            conflicts
           </a>
           , with increased
           <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">
            crime
           </a>
           and
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">
            warfare
           </a>
           . The multifarious, irreversible damage from global warming will accelerate as environmental
           <a href="/wiki/Tipping_point_(climatology)" title="Tipping point (climatology)">
            tipping points
           </a>
           are reached.
           <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
            Physicist
           </a>
           –
           <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">
            cosmologist
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">
            Stephen Hawking
           </a>
           on 2 July 2017, belatedly celebrating his 75th birthday, warned that
           <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
            planet Earth
           </a>
           is rapidly approaching an irreversible tipping point that will leave the planet with an uninhabitable environment like that of
           <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
            Venus
           </a>
           , with a temperature of 250 degrees and
           <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">
            sulfuric-acid rain
           </a>
           .
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
            <a href="#cite_note-149">
             [146]
            </a>
           </sup>
           The
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._military" title="U.S. military">
            U.S. military
           </a>
           are already forced to factor global-warming effects into their planning for military
           <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">
            infrastructure
           </a>
           , war, and
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disaster_relief" title="Disaster relief">
            disaster relief
           </a>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

         <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEBainesMalek20008-4">

            <a href="#cite_ref-72">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Zapotec civilization has its beginnings in 700 BCE: see
           <cite class="citation book">
            Flannery, Kent V.; Marcus, Joyce (1996).
            <i>
             Zapotec Civilization: How Urban Society Evolved in Mexico's Oaxaca Valley
            </i>
            . New York: Thames &amp; Hudson. p. 146.
            <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
             ISBN
            </a>
            <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-500-05078-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-500-05078-3">
             0-500-05078-3
            </a>
            .
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Zapotec+Civilization%3A+How+Urban+Society+Evolved+in+Mexico%27s+Oaxaca+Valley&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=146&amp;rft.pub=Thames+%26+Hudson&amp;rft.date=1996&amp;rft.isbn=0-500-05078-3&amp;rft.aulast=Flannery&amp;rft.aufirst=Kent+V.&amp;rft.au=Marcus%2C+Joyce&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+world">
            <span style="display:none;">
            </span>
           </span>
           Zapotec civilization ended in 1521 according to the five archaeological stages presented in
           <cite class="citation book">
            Whitecotton, Joseph W. (1977).
            <i>
             The Zapotecs: Princes, Priests, and Peasants
            </i>
            . Norman: University of Oklahoma Press. 26, LI.1–3.
           </cite>
           <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Zapotecs%3A+Princes%2C+Priests%2C+and+Peasants&amp;rft.place=Norman&amp;rft.pages=26%2C+LI.1-3&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Oklahoma+Press&amp;rft.date=1977&amp;rft.aulast=Whitecotton&amp;rft.aufirst=Joseph+W.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+world">

         <li id="cite_note-143">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-143">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The socioeconomic dislocations have prompted, in several countries, proposals for, and experiments with,
           <a href="/wiki/Basic_income" title="Basic income">
            citizens' guaranteed basic incomes
           </a>
           , which had been proposed in
           <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">
            Thomas Paine
           </a>
           's pamphlet,
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_Justice" title="Agrarian Justice">
             Agrarian Justice
            </a>
           </i>
           , published in 1797. The proposals' purposes and prospects are discussed in
           <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_M._Friedman" title="Benjamin M. Friedman">
            Benjamin M. Friedman
           </a>
           , "Born to Be Free" (review of
           <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Van_Parijs" title="Philippe Van Parijs">
            Philippe Van Parijs
           </a>
           and Yannick Vanderborght,
           <i>
            Basic Income: A Radical Proposal for a Free Society and a Sane Economy
           </i>
           ,
           <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Press" title="Harvard University Press">
            Harvard University Press
           </a>
           , 2017),
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">
             The New York Review of Books
            </a>
           </i>
           , vol. LXIV, no. 15 (12 October 2017), pp. 39–41.
          </span>

        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Diagrammatic+Chart+of+World+History&amp;rft.pub=Editions+Sides&amp;rft.date=1986&amp;rft.isbn=978-2-868-61096-6&amp;rft.au=Fournet%2C+Louis-Henri&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+world">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        <cite class="citation book">
         <a href="/wiki/Peter_Frankopan" title="Peter Frankopan">
          Frankopan, Peter
         </a>
         (2015).
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Silk_Roads:_A_New_History_of_the_World" title="The Silk Roads: A New History of the World">
           The Silk Roads: A New History of the World
          </a>
         </i>
         . Knopf.
         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
          ISBN
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-101-94632-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-101-94632-9">
          978-1-101-94632-9
         </a>
         .
        </cite>
        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Silk+Roads%3A+A+New+History+of+the+World&amp;rft.pub=Knopf&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-101-94632-9&amp;rft.au=Frankopan%2C+Peter&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHistory+of+the+world">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li>
        Jaffe, Amy Myers, "Green Giant: Renewable Energy and Chinese Power",
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Affairs" title="Foreign Affairs">
          Foreign Affairs
         </a>
        </i>
        , vol. 97, no. 2 (March / April 2018), pp. 83–93.
        <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
         China
        </a>
        is well on its way to "becom[ing] the
        <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">
         renewable energy
        </a>
        superpower of the future." (p. 84) China already generates 24% of its power from renewable sources; the United States generates 15% (p. 87). Over 100 Chinese companies now make electric cars and buses; China's
        <a href="/wiki/BYD_Auto" title="BYD Auto">
         BYD Auto
        </a>
        is the largest producer of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_vehicles" title="Electric vehicles">
         electric vehicles
        </a>
        in the world (p. 87). China has over a million electric cars on its roads—almost double the number in the United States (p. 87).
       </li>
       <li>
        <cite class="citation book">
         <a href="/wiki/David_Landes" title="David Landes">

         <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">

         </a>
